The most common types of host-guest interactions used in IDA design include:\n\n1. Hydrogen Bonding: Hosts such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and cucurbiturils can form hydrogen bonds with guest molecules (indicators or analytes), enabling selective binding and displacement.\n2. Electrostatic Interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., crown ethers, calixarenes with charged substituents) can interact with oppositely charged guests, allowing for displacement upon analyte introduction.\n3. π-π Stacking: Aromatic hosts (e.g., pillararenes, calixarenes) can engage in π-π interactions with aromatic indicators or analytes, which can be disrupted or replaced by a competing guest.\n4. Hydrophobic Effects: Hosts with hydrophobic cavities (e.g., cyclodextrins) can encapsulate hydrophobic indicators, which are displaced by more strongly binding analytes.\n5. Metal Coordination: Hosts containing metal centers (e.g., metallosupramolecular cages) can coordinate with indicator ligands, which are displaced by analytes with higher affinity for the metal center.\n\nIn all cases, the displacement of the indicator by the analyte leads to a measurable optical change, such as a shift in absorbance (color change) or fluorescence intensity, providing a convenient readout for analyte detection. The choice of host-guest interaction depends on the chemical nature of the analyte, the indicator, and the desired selectivity and sensitivity of the assay.', 'key_concepts': The most common types of host-guest interactions used in IDA design include:\n\n1. Hydrogen Bonding: Hosts such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and cucurbiturils can form hydrogen bonds with guest molecules (indicators or analytes), enabling selective binding and displacement.\n2. Electrostatic Interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., crown ethers, calixarenes with charged substituents) can interact with oppositely charged guests, allowing for displacement upon analyte introduction.\n3. π-π Stacking: Aromatic hosts (e.g., pillararenes, calixarenes) can engage in π-π interactions with aromatic indicators or analytes, which can be disrupted or replaced by a competing guest.\n4. Hydrophobic Effects: Hosts with hydrophobic cavities (e.g., cyclodextrins) can encapsulate hydrophobic indicators, which are displaced by more strongly binding analytes.\n5. Metal Coordination: Hosts containing metal centers (e.g., metallosupramolecular cages) can coordinate with indicator ligands, which are displaced by analytes with higher affinity for the metal center.\n\nIn all cases, the displacement of the indicator by the analyte leads to a measurable optical change, such as a shift in absorbance (color change) or fluorescence intensity, providing a convenient readout for analyte detection. The choice of host-guest interaction depends on the chemical nature of the analyte, the indicator, and the desired selectivity and sensitivity of the assay.', 'key_concepts': The most common types of host-guest interactions used in IDA design include:\n\n1. Hydrogen Bonding: Hosts such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and cucurbiturils can form hydrogen bonds with guest molecules (indicators or analytes), enabling selective binding and displacement.\n2. Electrostatic Interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., crown ethers, calixarenes with charged substituents) can interact with oppositely charged guests, allowing for displacement upon analyte introduction.\n3. π-π Stacking: Aromatic hosts (e.g., pillararenes, calixarenes) can engage in π-π interactions with aromatic indicators or analytes, which can be disrupted or replaced by a competing guest.\n4. Hydrophobic Effects: Hosts with hydrophobic cavities (e.g., cyclodextrins) can encapsulate hydrophobic indicators, which are displaced by more strongly binding analytes.\n5. Metal Coordination: Hosts containing metal centers (e.g., metallosupramolecular cages) can coordinate with indicator ligands, which are displaced by analytes with higher affinity for the metal center.\n\nIn all cases, the displacement of the indicator by the analyte leads to a measurable optical change, such as a shift in absorbance (color change) or fluorescence intensity, providing a convenient readout for analyte detection. The choice of host-guest interaction depends on the chemical nature of the analyte, the indicator, and the desired selectivity and sensitivity of the assay.', 'key_concepts': Hydrogen Bonding: Hosts such as cyclodextrins, calixarenes, and cucurbiturils can form hydrogen bonds with guest molecules (indicators or analytes), enabling selective binding and displacement.\n2. Electrostatic Interactions: Charged hosts (e.g., crown ethers, calixarenes with charged substituents) can interact with oppositely charged guests, allowing for displacement upon analyte introduction.\n3. π-π Stacking: Aromatic hosts (e.g., pillararenes, calixarenes) can engage in π-π interactions with aromatic indicators or analytes, which can be disrupted or replaced by a competing guest.\n4.
